  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/529935110" />
    <a title="Lexie Robinson Austin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3056234-lexie-robinson-austin"><img alt="Lexie Robinson Austin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360248304p2/3056234.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/529935110?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lexie Robinson Austin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lexie Robinson Austin" href="/user/show/3056234-lexie-robinson-austin">Lexie Robinson Austin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer529935110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12698847045811546298">I put off reading this book for a number of reasons. Its nonfiction, and I don’t really care for nonfiction in general. It’s about Atomic Bombs, and really, who wants to read about that? It’s all war-like and violent and stuff. And plus, the cover! Snoozefest. But then, it won the Newbery Honor Award. And the Siebert Award. And the YALSA Award for Excellence in Nonfiction for Young Adults. And The 2012 Washington Post Best Kids Books of the Year. And was a 2012 National Book Awards finalist for </span>
  <span id="freeText12698847045811546298" style="display:none">I put off reading this book for a number of reasons. Its nonfiction, and I don’t really care for nonfiction in general. It’s about Atomic Bombs, and really, who wants to read about that? It’s all war-like and violent and stuff. And plus, the cover! Snoozefest. But then, it won the Newbery Honor Award. And the Siebert Award. And the YALSA Award for Excellence in Nonfiction for Young Adults. And The 2012 Washington Post Best Kids Books of the Year. And was a 2012 National Book Awards finalist for Young People’s Literature. Okay, so maybe there is something to this book after all. So I picked it up. <br /><br />And y’all….it knocked my socks off.<br /><br />You plunge right into the story of the Atomic race with the capture of spy, Harry Gold. Harry sets the scene for the rest of the book which is filled with spies, intrigue, crazy sabotage schemes, planned kidnappings, explosions, and yes: history and science. The pure genius of this book is that Sheinkin is able to wrap history and science up in exciting, heart pounding plot lines so that this book reads like a Michael Bay action movie. <br /><br />Now, don’t be put off by the prospect of violence and war stuff in this book. Because, yes, it’s there. But Sheinkin doesn’t shy away from the awfulness of war. His description of Hiroshima is particularly blood chilling. He describes the scientists, who after the first test at Trinity, realized the potential for what they had created and vomited in the bushes. He does a great job of balancing out the pro-arms race fervor with pointed anecdotes, such as Oppenheimer’s visit and appeal to Harry Truman to stop creating atomic weapons. He even balances out the Spies for the USSR with the pointed question, “If the United States and USSR were allies against Hitler, why did the US hide all of their atomic research from the USSR?” He never really answers that question, but leaves the reader to surmise on their own. It paints the picture of scientists who were really conflicted about the nature of science, and that it should be shared instead of kept classified. Obviously trading national secrets to another country is a No No, but I think it was much harder for these scientists than I ever realized. <br /><br />One thing I found intriguing about this book as a selection for the Newbery Honor award is the potential controversy that could surround some of the language in the book. If you remember, there was HUGE amounts of controversy when The Higher Power of Lucky by Susan Patron won the Newbery Award in 2007 because it used the word “scrotum”. (Remember “Scrotumgate”, anyone?) It used it anatomically correctly, and in a totally appropriate setting. Like Lucky, in Bomb, there are some words that parents may not like. But they’re a little harsher. There is a “Son of a B@#$%” quote (historically accurate from a primary source) and reference to the F word as it pertains to mispronunciations of Karl Fuchs last name. But I haven’t heard anything about it. Not that I’m trying to stir anything up, I just keep wondering when that’s going to hit. <br /><br />All that aside, please know that this is a spectacular piece of history/science/narrative that made me feel like a better person for reading. I feel like I know my country better and I can understand that particular era of history better than I did before. And I emphasize, it was presented in such a way, that even someone like me who has the attention span of a gnat was engrossed. I’m honestly waiting to hear news that a movie studio has bought it up. <br /><br />It’s that good.<br /><br />Sidenote: Much like the right wine selection can enhance a good meal (those who know me well know the irony in this statement), I believe the right music selection can enhance a literary experience. If you are searching for the perfect soundtrack for Bomb, I recommend the CD Night Visions by Imagine Dragons. I happened to pick it up at the same time I was reading Bomb, and found it fit together very nicely. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12698847045811546298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/378822-wendy?shelf=2012-award-possibilities">2012-award-possibilities</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/378822-wendy?shelf=wwii">wwii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/378822-wendy?shelf=newbery-honor">newbery-honor</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440376017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12398078720890386980">This book is probably the greatest disappointment of the year to me. Because up until page 227, it was a five star book. I was ready to proclaim it the book of the year, the clear Newbery choice, a must-read.<br/><br/>But the epilogue--it's bad.<br/><br/>It would probably be going too far to say that it's actually poorly written. In comparison to most of the books that I've read this year, it's probably still in the top half. But the contrast between the epilogue and the rest of the book is so stark--and it leaves </span>
  <span id="freeText12398078720890386980" style="display:none">This book is probably the greatest disappointment of the year to me. Because up until page 227, it was a five star book. I was ready to proclaim it the book of the year, the clear Newbery choice, a must-read.<br /><br />But the epilogue--it's bad.<br /><br />It would probably be going too far to say that it's actually poorly written. In comparison to most of the books that I've read this year, it's probably still in the top half. But the contrast between the epilogue and the rest of the book is so stark--and it leaves such a bad taste in my mouth because it's at the end--that the impact on me as a reader is great.<br /><br />I don't know why Sheinkin decided to write the epilogue in more casual language than the rest of the book. I don't know why he suddenly started speaking to the reader instead of letting the book speak. I don't know why the editor let that pass (or maybe it was the editor's idea, who knows). But it very effectively dampened my enthusiasm for the book. The last page is particularly egregious. I really don't think young readers need a lecture about how scary nuclear warfare is. Not after reading the rest of the book.<br /><br />Is this still the best book of the year? Sigh--yes. Probably. Sure. --is what I'm saying now instead of the "YES! YES!" I was saying eleven pages earlier.<br /><br />I'll say the other things I was planning to say, too, also without the eagerness and excitement I was feeling. This is a great and fascinating accompaniment not only to THE GREEN GLASS SEA, but also to CODE NAME VERITY. It is so intense that I had to stop reading it sometimes and do something else. The author sets up and resolves many exciting plots on his way to the end of the big plot thread, and it's very effective. He uses suspenseful chapter and section endings effectively without making me feel manipulated, just fascinated.<br /><br />Maybe, eventually, I will be able to put the epilogue in perspective. You'll notice I only downgraded it by one star. But, as I said: a great disappointment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12398078720890386980" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_483442685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/483442685" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1222379-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242269090p2/1222379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/483442685?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/1222379-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1222379-barbara?shelf=community">community</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1222379-barbara?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1222379-barbara?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1222379-barbara?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer483442685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18310947406220336432">This mesmerizing account of the creation of the first atomic bomb describes three interconnected plot lines that follow the Allies' efforts to prevent the Germans from creating an the atomic bomb, the Americans' efforts to build the bomb, and the Soviets' determination to obtain the plans being used by American scientists. Throughout this engaging title, readers encounter vividly drawn personalities who make mistakes and are filled with regrets as well as a large cast of heroic figures. The desc</span>
  <span id="freeText18310947406220336432" style="display:none">This mesmerizing account of the creation of the first atomic bomb describes three interconnected plot lines that follow the Allies' efforts to prevent the Germans from creating an the atomic bomb, the Americans' efforts to build the bomb, and the Soviets' determination to obtain the plans being used by American scientists. Throughout this engaging title, readers encounter vividly drawn personalities who make mistakes and are filled with regrets as well as a large cast of heroic figures. The description of the first test bomb in New Mexico is filled with awe and wonder but also with shock and fear at what might come next now that this power has been unleashed. The author introduces heroes and villains throughout the pages, but he somehow manages to make even the worst decisions understandable. The influence of politics and the cost of human lives after bombs were dropped on Japan are described as well. Undoubtedly, no reader can fail to be moved by this account and these words that describe the mingled pride and fear of those involved in the Manhattan Project: "It was the chill of knowing that they had used something they loved--the study of physics--to build the deadliest weapon in human history" (p. 184). Lines such as this one are peppered in the book's pages, prompting reflection on humankind's potential for destruction and making readers such as me wonder how many of those scientists would have taken back all that they had learned and used to create the bomb if they could have. This well-written title will stay with readers long after they've finished reading it. What is more, I suspect many readers will want to read even more about the heroes and spies introduced here. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18310947406220336432" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16415293566634463943">I thought that non-fiction books could be very interesting and full of cool fun facts but I was certain that no non-fiction book could be informative,fast-paced, exciting, humorous and have a great plot line all at the same time.<br/>Oh boy did this book prove me wrong. <br/>Bomb is an amazing story about how a group of physicists made the first atomic bomb.   <span id="freeText16415293566634463943" style="display:none">I thought that non-fiction books could be very interesting and full of cool fun facts but I was certain that no non-fiction book could be informative,fast-paced, exciting, humorous and have a great plot line all at the same time.<br />Oh boy did this book prove me wrong. <br />Bomb is an amazing story about how a group of physicists made the first atomic bomb. It has a few diagrams,multiple pictures of the scientists and letters at the end. There are so many ‘characters’ (real-life people) but Steve Sheinkin manages to make sure the reader remembers them by dropping in subtle reminders. It also has the actual scientists’ very funny conversations. <br /><br />This book has a great plot, you learn lots about the history of the atomic bomb and many scientists, and it’s a definite page-turner. I recommend it to everyone, no matter what genres you like. It’s a must-read.</span>

<div id="review_2040400207" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2040400207" />
    <a title="Nathan Hipple" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111279-nathan-hipple"><img alt="Nathan Hipple" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580433021p2/56111279.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2040400207?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan Hipple" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan Hipple" href="/user/show/56111279-nathan-hipple">Nathan Hipple</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2040400207" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4735481708623819759">You know you're reading a good narrative non-fiction books when you are heartbroken by historical events you already knew were going to happen. You find yourself hoping that somehow you were wrong, that somehow it won't turn out that way. Maybe the Americans won't leak the plans about the atomic bomb to the Soviets? Maybe the government won't turn on Oppenheimer? And then, it crushes you. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1159940795?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elisabeth Cody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elisabeth Cody" href="/user/show/35048290-elisabeth-cody">Elisabeth Cody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1159940795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2425254271871254992">BOMB id the race to build and steal the worlds biggest atomic bomb built after the discovery of fission, the discovery of atoms splitting in two, and the people involved in it. It also features famous physicists that lived back then (Albert Einstein, Robert Oppenheimer) and is a great history learning experience. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('faig+7IzNc8mwUAD8LQeolbF1V9a/+mfyXUfXRi1FCiu6/uqJ15HOJpOp7XypZcGROW5fiJxjTfqAmegrbGojg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iWmpO2RnidgNpQ0NF3T2Z8O4ycrjBsQZVAxTOpjeQkhaKvJq8Qr7L7Eq6rsVZX/D0Zil65uIoLF3eyvHLdr+7g==')})
  }

This is incorrect. Includes step-by-step instructions, and clever illustrations that make the system easy to understand. Explains how to cope with recessions and avoid unemployment.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/480253.Steve_Sheinkin">About Steve Sheinkin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/480253.Steve_Sheinkin"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1311581477p3/480253.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/480253.Steve_Sheinkin">Steve Sheinkin
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
432
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12840233540360239601">From: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://stevesheinkin.com/about/'>http://stevesheinkin.com/about/</a><br/><br/>I was born in Brooklyn, NY, and my family lived in Mississippi and Colorado before moving back to New York and settling in the suburbs north of New York City. As a kid my favorite books were action stories and outdoor adventures: sea stories, searches for buried treasure, sharks eating people… that kind of thing. Probably my all-time favorite was a book called </span>
  <span id="freeText12840233540360239601" style="display:none">From: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://stevesheinkin.com/about/">http://stevesheinkin.com/about/</a><br /><br />I was born in Brooklyn, NY, and my family lived in Mississippi and Colorado before moving back to New York and settling in the suburbs north of New York City. As a kid my favorite books were action stories and outdoor adventures: sea stories, searches for buried treasure, sharks eating people… that kind of thing. Probably my all-time favorite was a book called Mutiny on the Bounty, a novel based on the true story of a famous mutiny aboard a British ship in the late 1700s.<br /><br />I went to Syracuse University and studied communications and international relations. The highlight of those years was a summer I spent in Central America, where I worked on a documentary on the streets of Nicaragua.<br /><br />After college I moved to Washington, D.C., and worked for an environmental group called the National Audubon Society. Then, when my brother Ari graduated from college a few years later, we decided to move to Austin, Texas, and make movies together. We lived like paupers in a house with a hole in the floor where bugs crawled in. We wrote some screenplays, and in 1995 made our own feature film, a comedy called A More Perfect Union (filing pictured below), about four young guys who decide to secede from the Union and declare their rented house to be an independent nation. We were sure it was going to be a huge hit; actually we ended up deep in debt.<br /><br />After that I moved to Brooklyn and decided to find some way to make a living as a writer. I wrote short stories, screenplays, and worked on a comic called The Adventures of Rabbi Harvey. In 2006, after literally hundreds of rejections, my first Rabbi Harvey graphic novel was finally published.<br /><br />Meanwhile, I started working for an educational publishing company, just for the money. We’d hire people to write history textbooks, and they’d send in their writing, and it was my job to check facts and make little edits to clarify the text. Once in a while I was given the chance to write little pieces of textbooks, like one-page biographies or skills lessons. “Understanding Bar Graphs” was one of my early works. The editors noticed that my writing was pretty good. They started giving me less editing to do, and more writing. Gradually, I began writing chapters for textbooks, and that turned into my full-time job. All the while, I kept working on my own writing projects.<br /><br />In 2008 I wrote my last textbook. I walked away, and shall never return. My first non-textbook history book was King George: What Was His Problem? – full of all the stories about the American Revolution that I was never allowed to put into textbooks. But looking back, I actually feel pretty lucky to have spent all those years writing textbooks. It forced me to write every day, which is great practice. And I collected hundreds of stories that I can’t wait to tell.<br /><br />These days, I live with my wife, Rachel, and our two young kids in Saratoga Springs, New York. We’re right down the road from the Saratoga National Historical Park, the site of Benedict Arnold’s greatest – and last – victory in an American uniform. But that’s not why I moved here. Honestly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12840233540360239601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/18349336-bomb-the-race-to-build-and-steal-the-world-s-most-dangerous-weapon">Quotes from Bomb: The Race to...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;If you think atomic explosions in Asia wouldn't affect Americans, consider this. A study published in Scientific American in 2010 looked at the probable impact of a "small" nuclear war, one in which India and Pakistan each dropped fifty atomic bombs. The scientists concluded that the explosions would ignite massive firestorms, sending enormous amounts of dust and smoke into the atmosphere. This would block some of the sun's light from reaching the earth, making the planet colder and darker - for about ten years. Farming would collapse, and people all over the globe would starve to death. And that's if only half of one percent of all the atomic bombs on earth were used.<br /><br />In the end, this is a difficult story to sum up. The making of the atomic bomb is one of history's most amazing examples of teamwork and genius and poise under pressure. But it's also the story of how humans created a weapon capable of wiping our species off the planet. It's a story with no end in sight.<br /><br />And, like it or not, you're in it.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
